and Canadian English), also called American checkers or Straight checkers, is a form of the strategy board game draughts\r\n\r\nMove rules\r\nThere are two different ways to move in English draughts:\r\nA simple move consists of moving a piece one square diagonally to an adjacent unoccupied dark square. Uncrowned pieces can move only diagonally forward; kings can move in any diagonal direction.\r\nA jump consists of moving a piece from a square diagonally adjacent to an opponent's piece, to an empty square immediately beyond it in the same direction. (Thus jumping over the opponent's piece.) Uncrowned pieces can jump only diagonally forward; kings can jump in any diagonal direction. A jumped piece is considered \"captured\" and removed from the game. Any piece, whether crowned or not, can jump a king.\r\nMultiple jumps are possible, if after one jump, another piece is immediately eligible to be jumped‚Äîeven if that jump is in a different diagonal direction. If more than one multiple-jump move is available, the player can choose which piece to jump with, and which sequence of jumps to make. The jumping sequence chosen is not required to be the one that maximizes the number of jumps in the turn; however, a player must make all available jumps in the sequence chosen.\r\nJumping is always mandatory: if a player has the option to jump, he must take it, even if doing so results in disadvantage for the jumping player. For example, a mandated single jump might set up the player such that the opponent has a multi-jump in reply.\r\n\r\nThe best board game that does not require any special representation.
